    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="57a40b234bba48cd9d3915e0ae08c091">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7b621fd2-9d77-4a87-b25a-f53f66891f6a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Contribution: Conceptualization (equal), Funding acquisition (lead), Investigation (supporting), Methodology (supporting), Project administration (lead), Resources (lead), Supervision (lead), Validation (equal), Writing - review &amp; editing (equal)</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Manthiram/Arumugam">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d101962791" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>An effective reduction of multiple phase transformations in NaNiO<sub>2</sub> has been achieved through divalent Mg<sup>2+</sup> doping, which deshields the electron density from Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This outcome can be attributed to the more ionic nature of MgO bond, creating free space for the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and mitigating the Na<sup>+</sup>ion ordering.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/305a599a-7af3-428e-a88e-489e9e4f7818/anie202403865-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/305a599a-7af3-428e-a88e-489e9e4f7818/anie202403865-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ee5a14b2-3486-41f2-a664-bf1cef292e65/anie202403865-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/305a599a-7af3-428e-a88e-489e9e4f7818/anie202403865-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d101962794" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The O-type layered nickel oxides suffer from undesired cooperative JahnTeller distortion stemming from Ni<sup>3+</sup> ions and undergo multiple biphasic structural transformations during the insertion/extraction of large Na<sup>+</sup> ions, posing a significant challenge to stabilize the structural integrity. We present here a systematic investigation of the impact of substituting 5% divalent (Mg<sup>2+</sup>) or trivalent (Al<sup>3+</sup> or Co<sup>3+</sup>) ions for Ni<sup>3+</sup> to alleviate Na<sup>+</sup>ion ordering and perturb the JahnTeller effect to enhance structural stability. We gauge a fundamental understanding of the MgO and NaO or MgONa bonding interactions, noting that the ionicity of the MgO bond deshields the electronic cloud of oxygen from Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Furthermore, calculations of the Van Vleck distortion modes reveal a relaxation of NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra from JahnTeller distortion and a reduced electron density at the interlayer with Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitution. Long-range (<i>operando</i></p>
            <p>X-ray diffraction) and short-range (magic angle spinning nuclear magnetic resonance) structural analyses provide insights into reduced ordering, allowing a stable continuous solid solution. Overall, Mg-substitution results in a high-capacity retention of ~96% even after 100cycles, showcasing the potential of this strategy for overcoming the structural instabilities and enhancing the performance of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403865-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403865-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Crystal chemistry unfolds in a captivating interplay as sodium partners with the 3<i>d</i> transition-metal ions, M=Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, and Ni, to form the layered NaMO<sub>2</sub> oxides. The layered sodium oxides present a compelling alternative to lithium analogs, reducing the synthesis complexities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0001_R_d101962782e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Phys. B+C">1</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0003_R_d101962782e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Solid State Chem.">3</a></sup></span> This alternative aims to diversify the market, thereby reducing the strain on the supply chain and the cost of Li-ion batteries (LIBs). In contrast to lithium, which forms the layered LiMO<sub>2</sub> structure only with a few 3<i>d</i> transition metals, the analogous sodium crystal chemistry stands out for its vast compositional landscape. The inherent drawbacks associated with sodium, such as larger size, lower cell voltage, and structural instabilities associated with multiple phase transformations, have been hampering their practical application.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0004_R_d101962782e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0005_R_d101962782e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span> It is critical to understand the fundamental chemistry and limitations of these materials to make rapid progress towards adopting sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) for vehicle and grid storage applications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0006_R_d101962782e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Energy">6</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Although different types of layered NaMO<sub>2</sub> structures are known, the literature has largely focused on P2-type materials for NIBs due to their better stability and kinetics. Unfortunately, these materials are Na-deficient with Na contents of &lt;0.8 and exhibit multiple phase transformations during chargedischarge cycling. To overcome the detrimental phase transformations, doping with other transition-metal ions (&gt;10%) have been pursued, further reducing the effective Na content in the material during synthesis.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0007_R_d101962782e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0009_R_d101962782e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span> Moreover, these materials exhibit moderate cell voltages, limiting the energy density. These limitations have generated much interest recently towards O3 type NaMO<sub>2</sub> oxides. Startup companies, such as HiNa and Faradion, employ, respectively, O3Na<sub>0.9</sub>Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and O3Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>1xyz</sub>Mg<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>Ti<sub>z</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0010_R_d101962782e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0011_R_d101962782e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></sup></span> Synthesis of O3NaNiO<sub>2</sub> is straight forward with the ideal Na:Ni ratio of 1:1 due to the higher electropositive character of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02) and the larger size compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76) and Ni<sup>3+</sup> (0.56). As a result, the O3NaNiO<sub>2</sub> adopts distorted monoclinic (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>) symmetry at room temperature due to the ferrodistorsive orbital ordering caused by the JahnTeller active low-spin Ni<sup>3+</sup>: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>6</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup>, while the ideal rhombohedral symmetry exists at higher temperatures of &gt;450K.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0012_R_d101962782e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Joule">12</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0014_R_d101962782e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Eur. Phys. J. B">14</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> as a cathode have been investigated for Na-ion batteries, given its high theoretical capacity and relatively high operating voltages.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0015_R_d101962782e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Mater. Res. Bull.">15</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0017_R_d101962782e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span> Braconnier etal. initially explored the electrochemistry of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> by studying the phase transformations the voltage window of 1.8 to 3.4V revealed four distinct phase transformations (O3P3P3O3), with a further increase in voltage (~3.7V) leading to O3 phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0015_R_d101962782e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Mater. Res. Bull.">15</a></sup></span> Further, O3-type NaNi<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was synthesized as both rhombohedral (<i>R</i>-3<i>m</i>) and monoclinic (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>) phases, employing a quenching method. The monoclinic phase performed better than the rhombohedral phase, but the cobalt content and multiple phase transformations are still persistent issues.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0018_R_d101962782e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Power Sources">18</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0020_R_d101962782e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, the existing literature has not adequately addressed the issues of Na-ion ordering in layered oxide cathodes, leaving a huge gap in understanding the implications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0005_R_d101962782e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0011_R_d101962782e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0021_R_d101962782e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> Furthermore, we aim to maintain the dopant molecular weight as low as possible and ensure oxidation states are less than or equal to the Ni oxidation state. As the oxidation state increases, the dopant tends to predominantly cover the surface due to the tendency of these elements to form low coordination.</p>
         
         <p>We focus in this study on elucidating the underlying cause of sodium-ion ordering and instability at higher voltages by investigating a substitution of 5% of divalent (Mg<sup>2+</sup>) or trivalent (Al<sup>3+</sup> or Co<sup>3+</sup>) ions for Ni in NaNiO<sub>2</sub>. Notably, this substitution does not alter the Na content in the structure. The electron density mapping and calculation of Van Vleck distortions along with an examination of the migration pathways help improve the understanding of the structural changes. Extensive electrochemical analyses are conducted on these materials within two distinct voltage windows (2.0 to 4.0V and 1.8 to 4.2V). Utilizing <i>operando</i> X-ray diffraction and potentiostatic intermittent titration technique (PITT), we observe an alleviation of sodium ordering in Mg<sup>2+</sup>-doped samples with a reduction in JahnTeller distortions. <i>Operando</i> X-ray diffraction and galvanostatic intermitted titration technique (GITT) are carried out during the first two cycles to understand the irreversible phase transformations and evaluate the diffusion coefficient. Further, Magic angle spinning nuclear magnetic resonance (MASNMR ) spectroscopy provides insights into the local structural changes resulting from the dopant incorporations. Additionally, projection of magic-angle turning, and phased-adjusted sideband separation (pj-MATPASS) experiments offer further understanding of the distinct sites present for Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the presence of dopants. This comprehensive investigation sheds light on the intricate interplay among dopant-induced modifications, sodium-ion ordering, and the structural stability of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> at elevated voltages.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403865-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403865-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202403865-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202403865-sec-0003-title"> Synthesis, Crystal Structure, and Migration Pathways</h3>
            
            <p>O3 NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and 5% Mg, Al or Co doped NaNiO<sub>2</sub> (hereafter termed, respectively, as NM, NA, and NC for simplicity) samples were synthesized, for the first time, with a co-precipitation method, employing a two-step process (supporting note1). Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1a</a> illustrates a representative set of ambient temperature diffractograms for NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and the doped samples. These patterns were analyzed with Rietveld refinement using the standard patterns in the literature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0022_R_d101962782e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Powder Diffr.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0023_R_d101962782e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> The resulting lattice parameters are given in Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#anie202403865-tbl-0001">1</a>. There is a small variation in the positions of the (001) peaks due to the contraction or expansion of the crystal lattice depending on the size of the dopants. An enlarged version of the high intensity peaks at 16.9 is presented in FigureS1. There is an increase in the <i>d</i>-spacing for the divalent Mg-doped sample (5.24) and a slight decrease in the <i>d</i>-spacing for the trivalent Al- and Co-doped samples. This trend is consistent with the smaller ionic sizes of Al<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>3+</sup> compared to Mg<sup>2+</sup>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202403865-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2c823526-f938-42c3-9d01-cce0e61fafcd/anie202403865-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2c823526-f938-42c3-9d01-cce0e61fafcd/anie202403865-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8ec5c173-9184-4b00-a13d-a8e077db29c3/anie202403865-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2c823526-f938-42c3-9d01-cce0e61fafcd/anie202403865-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403865-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403865" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

               </figure>
            </section>

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The hexagonal symmetry of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> is strained, resulting in a monoclinic symmetry. This strain is attributed to the cooperative JahnTeller distortion induced by the low spin Ni<sup>3+</sup> ions (<i>t</i><sub>2<i>g</i></sub><sup>6</sup> <i>e<sub>g</sub></i><sup>1</sup>). As transition-metal and oxide ions are bonded octahedrally to form a hexagonal lattice, the lattice parameters can be related as <i>a</i> (monoclinic)=3<i>a</i> (hexagonal) and <i>b</i> monoclinic=<i>a</i> (hexagonal).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0024_R_d101962782e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Synth. Met.">24</a></sup></span> The expected <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> ratio in the lattice for hexagonal symmetry is 3 (1.73); we see a deviation with the value exceeding 1.86 in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1b</a>, in which the NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra share edges with adjacent octahedra to form NiO<sub>2</sub> sheets (transition-metal (TM) layer). The Na-ions are sandwiched between the NiO<sub>2</sub> sheets, forming edge-shared NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. The scanning electron micrographs (SEM) in Figure1c-g show the spherical particles with a uniform size distribution for NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, NA, NM and NC. Further, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) confirms a uniform distribution of the dopants throughout the particles (detailed mapping FigureS2). The substitution of trivalent Co<sup>3+</sup> or Al<sup>3+</sup> for trivalent Ni<sup>3+</sup> can be accomplished without causing many changes in the Ni-layer. However, substitution of the divalent ion Mg<sup>2+</sup> leads to an expansion of the lattice and a reduction in the electronic cloud within the transition-metal layer due to the larger ionic radius of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72) compared to Ni<sup>3+</sup> (0.56).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0007_R_d101962782e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span> The charge imbalance could cause vacancies and defects in the lattice.</p>
            
            <p>The single electron in Ni<sup>3+</sup>:<i>e<sub>g</sub></i><sup><i>1</i></sup> leads to a cooperative JahnTeller distortion, resulting in an elongation of the NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra in the z-direction. The in-plane NiO (<i>xy</i>-plane) bond length and the out-of-plane NiO (z-direction) bond length ratio is 0.9. Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1h</a> presents the bond lengths, highlighting the strain in monoclinic O3NaNiO<sub>2</sub> structure. The migration pathways were calculated with the <i>softBV</i> software tool, employing the bond valence pathway analyzer (BVPA) for automated pathway analysis to understand the mobility dynamics of movable ions (supporting note 1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0025_R_d101962782e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Mater.">25</a></sup></span> This tool provides a visualization of ion pathways in the crystal structure, at a spatial resolution of &lt;0.1. The initial energy for the Na-ion site is notably high, indicating structural instabilities for the Na-ion site at ambient conditions, moisture sensitivity, and the highly reactive nature of the system. The structural images depict the layer arrangements in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1i</a> and the potential percolation pathways of the ions through the neighboring tetrahedral sites. The red dotted lines in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1i</a> are all various possible migration pathways for the ions. It also clearly shows that the O-type systems follow the zig-zag pathways to hop from one site to the other. The energy barriers for NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and the doped samples vary over the scale of 0.705 to 0.751eV, with the Mg<sup>2+</sup> doped sample exhibiting a lower energy barrier of 0.705eV. The predominant pathways are 1D, given that 2D pathways (&gt;0.85eV) are constrained by a higher migration barrier (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0001">1j</a>). All the structural illustrations were generated using VESTA software.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0026_R_d101962782e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">26</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202403865-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202403865-sec-0004-title"> Bonding Interactions and Distortion Modes</h3>
            
            <p>Our investigation delves deeper into the impact of divalent Mg<sup>2+</sup> vs. trivalent Al<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>3+</sup> dopants to mitigate the Na-ion ordering. To elucidate the distinctiveness of Mg doping, we employ an approach based on the ionicity/covalency of MgO vs. NaO bonding interactions. Among the dopants studied, Mg<sup>2+</sup> lower electronegativity (1.31), and displays a heightened affinity for oxygen atoms compared to Na, Ni, Al and Co. The ionic MgO bond with a larger electronegativity difference between Mg and O (2.3) and a 2+ charge on Mg<sup>2+</sup> and 2 charge on O<sup>2</sup> suppresses the affinity of oxygen towards Na, leading to weaker bonding interaction between Na and O, despite the lower electronegativity of Na. The ionic MgO bond deshields oxygen affinity away from Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in an increased <i>d</i>-spacing and enhanced freedom of movement for Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The rigidity of the MgO bond acts as a stabilizing pillar, impeding the gliding of oxygen layers.</p>
            
            <p>To reinforce our assertions, we generated G-Fourier electron density maps from the refined crystallographic information files (CIFs). The G Fourier map is projected along the <i>ac</i>-plane of the monoclinic unit cell, where the intensities are directly implying the higher atomic number of atoms such that Ni represents the most intense peaks. For a better understanding, we overlay the monoclinic unit cell with atomic labels on the electron density map. Similar atoms are aligned in parallel to x-axis. The grey line represents the electronic distribution between the NaO and NiO bonds. The electron density between Na and O atoms in NM is lower than that in undoped NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and in the trivalent-ion-doped samples, measured from the <i>xz</i> plane, as shown in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0002">2a</a><a href="#anie202403865-fig-0002">d</a>. This provides direct evidence to understand the stronger ionic bonds between Mg and O and its influence in reducing the electronic cloud between Na and O.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202403865-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e73d41c-6036-4816-980d-916dc34b3e93/anie202403865-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e73d41c-6036-4816-980d-916dc34b3e93/anie202403865-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2049fea-e02a-458c-afd4-973f50aacb26/anie202403865-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e73d41c-6036-4816-980d-916dc34b3e93/anie202403865-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403865-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403865" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(ad) 3D Electron density calculated in the same plane for all the cathodes, showing the differences in electron density. (ef) Comparison of the Van Vleck distortion modes and magnitude.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Primarily, the electron movements occur within the NiONi framework, and introduction of Mg serves to mitigating the cooperative JahnTeller distortions by interrupting the long-range order.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0027_R_d101962782e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Mol. Cryst. Liq. Cryst. Sci. Technol. Sect. A">27</a></sup></span> To support the above argument, we have calculated the Van Vleck distortion modes to describe all possible distortions of NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. The room temperature monoclinic NaNiO<sub>2</sub> phase exhibits a significant distortion. To elucidate the influence of doping elements on these distortions, we employed <i>VanVleckCalculator</i> developed by Nagle-Cocco etal.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0028_R_d101962782e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">28</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0029_R_d101962782e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Chem. Phys.">29</a></sup></span> These modes describe all possible displacements, such as bonds (<i>Q</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>Q</i><sub>2</sub> and <i>Q</i><sub>3</sub>) or angles (<i>Q</i><sub>4</sub>, <i>Q</i><sub>5</sub> and <i>Q</i><sub>6</sub>) of NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. TableS1 delineates the physical interpretation of these modes within an octahedron. Notably, <i>Q</i><sub>3</sub> signifies z-elongation, the most probable distortion in NaNiO<sub>2</sub> or any cooperative JahnTeller active crystal systems. Using refined CIFs as inputs in a python code, the magnitude of distortions were calculated.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0030_R_d101962782e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></sup></span> The magnitudes of all the six distortions are decreased in NM. Surprisingly, all dopants reduced the octahedral shear distortion (<i>Q</i><sub>4</sub> to <i>Q</i><sub>6</sub>), but NC has more shear distortions than NM and NA. The reduction of <i>Q</i><sub>3</sub> in NC effectively improves the chemical stability of the compound, so we could reversibly extract/insert more Na ions. Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0002">2e</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0002">f</a> are a pictorial representation of the mentioned distortions (<i>Q</i><sub>1</sub> to <i>Q</i><sub>6</sub>). The electrochemical performance of the cathode materials with 5% dopants is much better than that of the undoped NaNiO<sub>2</sub>. However, the Na-ion ordering is still a major issue with NC and NA cathodes. Whenever there is a higher degree of covalency, the electron cloud distribution is uniform, as it is mutually shared; in contrast, as the ionicity increases, the electron cloud agglomerates more towards the electronegative atoms.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202403865-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202403865-sec-0005-title"> Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical evaluations of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and doped cathodes were conducted within two distinct potential ranges 1.8 to 4.2V and 2.0 to 4.0V. The deliberate operation of cathodes at elevated voltages was aimed to gain insights into structural stability, as previous studies have suggested structural destabilization above 3.75V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0016_R_d101962782e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span> Notably, our investigation revealed that the 5% dopants can effectively change the interaction between the atoms leading to good cycling performance between 2.0 and 4.0V. These electrodes were prepared with a dry polymer process involving polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE), as illustrated in the SEM image of the dry-processed electrodes provided in FigureS3. This process was implemented to mitigate the air-exposure problems during the wet casting process or possible moisture contamination from the NMP, which could lead to partial desodiation to form the more stable P3-type Na<sub>0.91</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub> phase. The electrochemical behavior of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> aligns well with previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0015_R_d101962782e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Mater. Res. Bull.">15</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0017_R_d101962782e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span> NaNiO<sub>2</sub> displays a multiple-step charge/discharge profile with five well-pronounced reversible plateaus.</p>
            
            <p>Each of these plateaus corresponds to a specific ordering of Na-ions in between the NiO<sub>2</sub> layers, attributed to the gliding of the oxygen planes as the structure transitions between O-type and P-type. The first cycle profiles of the cathodes are presented in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">d</a> in the potential range of 1.8 to 4.2V and 2.0 to 4.0V (multiple charge/discharge curves shown in FigureS4). There is an activation overpotential in NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and the tri-valent doped samples (denoted as dotted circle in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">d</a>) as evidenced by a sudden increase and then a drop in the potential after the charging process is initiated. However, the activation overpotential was not observed in the Mg<sup>2+</sup>-doped sample, implying a faster ion mobility with increased <i>d</i>-spacing as well as the absence of sharp ordering during desodiation. The discharge capacities in two different potential windows of 1.84.2V and 2.04.0V are, respectively, 146 and 116mAhg<sup>1</sup> for NM and 152 and 113mAhg<sup>1</sup> for NC, which are higher than those in both the potential windows for the undoped (116 and 102mAhg<sup>1</sup>) and NA (121 and 105mAhg<sup>1</sup>) samples. The charge/discharge capacities and capacity retention after 100cycles are summarized in TableS2. Further, the average operating voltage for NM is more than 200mV higher than those of the other cathodes. The differential capacity plots show sharp peaks for NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, NA, and NC, corresponding to the Ni redox (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3b</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">e</a>). However, these peaks are broader in NM and irrespective of the potential window, they exhibit smoother intercalation/deintercalation profiles. The differential capacity plots are presented to understand the specific potentials for the phase transformations. The electrochemistry in the potential window ranging from 1.8 to 4.2V indicates an irreversible redox peak located around 4.1V during the oxidation process (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3b</a>). However, the reduction peaks are non-equivalent, and it appears at 3.8V in NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NA during the discharge process. This may be related to structural deformations and poor cycling stability compared to those in NM and NC. The cycling data in both potential windows are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3c</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">f</a>. The capacity retention is &gt;76% for the NM, but 68%, 72%, and 66%, respectively, for the NC, NA, and NaNiO<sub>2</sub> samples in the potential range of 1.8 to 4.2V. Further, to avoid the irreversible reaction above 4V, we carried out the electrochemical evaluation at 2.0 to 4.0V. The four cathodes show more stable electrochemical performances in this window compared to the extended window. The capacity retention for NM after 100cycles is &gt;95%, which is around 20% higher than that in the extended potential window. The rate capability was also tested in both the potential windows from C/20 to 2C rates (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">3g</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0003">h</a>). Overall, NM exhibits better rate capability than all other cathodes. NC also exhibits better rate capability, but structural transformations due to Na-ion ordering are still very prominent and affect its long-term cyclability. The galvanostatic second cycle charge/discharge profiles for each cathode at different rates in both potential windows are presented in FiguresS5 and S6. Additionally, cycling stability for more than 200cycles is illustrated in FigureS6. Notably, NM demonstrates over 85% of capacity retention even after 200cycles.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202403865-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad8e4147-7a3e-4e73-ac1e-7bb2c0f9e956/anie202403865-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad8e4147-7a3e-4e73-ac1e-7bb2c0f9e956/anie202403865-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cad4e86b-22c5-410d-a5d0-1a7c83a39556/anie202403865-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad8e4147-7a3e-4e73-ac1e-7bb2c0f9e956/anie202403865-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202403865-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202403865-sec-0006-title"> <i>Operando</i> X-ray Diffraction and Potentiostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (PITT)</h3>
            
            <p>In order to understand the detailed structural chemistry, both the undoped NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and doped cathodes were investigated with <i>operando</i> X-ray diffraction. The peaks in the patterns coincide with five (<i>x</i>=1, 0.8, 2/3, 1/2 and 2/5) pronounced plateaus observed during the galvanostatic charge/discharge profile of Na<sub>x</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>. During cycling, the initial two hours rest showed two peaks in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4a</a>, one at 16.3 and 16.9. The peak at 16.3 corresponds to the evolution of the P3Na<sub>0.91</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0017_R_d101962782e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span> We have performed this test for the initial two cycles to understand the reversibility of the phases forming during the first cycle. It is consistent that the (00l) peak of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> shifts from 16.9 to 16.3 without returning to 16.9 during discharge. NaNiO<sub>2</sub> shows the co-existence of three phases during the initial plateau as denoted in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4a</a> with O3+P3+O3 phases. There is a sudden jump in the peak position aligned with the second plateau in the charge profile, which is termed as P3Na<sub>0.55</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>. It is even clearly visible in the other set of peaks located at 31.9, 37 and 38.3. Upon further charging, two new peaks appear, respectively at 36.69 and 41, while the intensity of the peak at 38.4 notably decreases. This region above 3.5V represents the phase transformation of P3 (Na<sub>0.5</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>) to O3 (Na<sub>0.4</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>) and then a subsequent transformation into O3 (Na<sub>0.37</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>). We can observe a similar trend in the NA sample as well; the first cycle (001) peak <i>operando</i> pattern is given in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4b</a>; the full patterns for the first two cycles are presented in FigureS7. In contrast, the situation is different in the case of both NM and NC. The Co doping provides good chemical stability by preventing the stronger cooperative JahnTeller distortion (see lower <i>Q</i><sub>3</sub>: Van Vleck mode of distortion). However, it still has different Na-ion ordering due to stronger NaO bonding interactions (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4c</a>). NC also exhibits similar phase transformations during the initial plateau, with less discrete transitions compared to NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NA.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202403865-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f3a3d6a2-cbad-42f8-88bf-316d814336cf/anie202403865-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f3a3d6a2-cbad-42f8-88bf-316d814336cf/anie202403865-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2366ad5a-cc2f-4c7b-8b9c-84edbdcc907e/anie202403865-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f3a3d6a2-cbad-42f8-88bf-316d814336cf/anie202403865-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403865-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403865" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) The first two cycles of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> <i>operando</i> XRD, showing the multiple phases matching with the charge/discharge plateaus (b) First cycle (001) peak <i>operando</i> X-ray diffraction of NA. (c) First cycle (001) peak <i>operando</i> X-ray diffraction of NC. (d) The first two cycles <i>operando</i> XRD of NM showing a smooth charge/discharge profile compared to NaNiO<sub>2</sub> (e) The enlarged version of the first charge profile of NM. (f) The <i>d</i>-spacing evaluation of the first charge profile of NM. (g and h) PITT profiles of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NM.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Interestingly, Mg doping effectively eliminates multiple phase transformations from O-type to P-type. Mg doping induces reversible structural and electronic changes in a more gradual manner during cycling. <i>Operando</i> X-ray diffraction reveals smooth transitions in the diffraction peaks rather than sudden leaps. Understanding the phase transformations during charge/discharge in NM is crucial to unravel the reason behind the stable electrochemical performance. Optimal Mg substitution in P2Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnMg<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> also demonstrated good electrochemical performance and elimination of multiple phase transitions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0009_R_d101962782e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0031_R_d101962782e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Electrochem. Commun.">31</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0032_R_d101962782e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span> Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4d</a> depicts the charge/discharge profiles and the corresponding <i>operando</i> X-ray contour map of NM for the first two cycles. Similar to NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, an initial biphasic domain with the coexistence of both the O-type and P-type phases (O3NaNM+P3Na<sub>0.91</sub>NM) is observed until the Na-content reaches x&lt;0.88.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the complexity increases in the system during the initial plateau region until x&lt;0.7, where three different phases coexist: O3 (O3NaNM)+P3 (P3Na<sub>0.91</sub>NM)+P3 (P3Na<sub>0.81</sub>NM), as presented in FigureS8.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0017_R_d101962782e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0033_R_d101962782e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> However, this triple phase domain is observed only during the first charge cycle. Two other strong Na-orderings correspond to <i>x</i>=2/3 (P3N<sub>0.66</sub>M) and <i>x</i>=<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/00cc6067-3bab-482f-ad5d-3e1a804bcdd9/anie202403865-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
 (P3Na<sub>0.5</sub>M). Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4e</a> provides an enlarged view of the <i>operando</i> XRD patterns during first charge to elucidate the shift in 2 and possible phase transformations. Attempts to analyze the patterns using LeBail refinement reveal that from x ~0.5 to complete charge x&lt;0.15, the lattice parameters do not undergo significant changes (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4f</a>). The peaks appearing at 42.1 and 42.8, after reaching <i>x</i>=0.5, maintain their positions throughout the full charge. In contrast, other cathodes exhibit simultaneous expansion and contraction of the lattice (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4</a> and FigureS7).</p>
            
            <p>Upon charging, the average oxidation state of Ni increases (Ni<sup>+4</sup>), effectively reducing the JahnTeller active metal centers and simplifying the structure, as observed in <i>operando</i> diffraction. It can also be attributed to the Ni<sup>+4</sup>/Mg<sup>2+</sup> ordering in the lattice, resulting in super structure peaks in the <i>operando</i> patterns (FigureS8 and FigureS9).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0033_R_d101962782e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> Two solid-solution domains with defined ordering at <i>x</i>=0.66 (P3Na<sub>0.66</sub>NM) and <i>x</i>=0.51 (P3Na<sub>0.51</sub>NM) were identified through sequential Rietveld analysis. Sequential Rietveld analysis was performed to discern the trend in the lattice parameters and volume changes between <i>x</i>=0.76 to 0.18 (during charge in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4f</a> and during discharge in FigureS9). This window of Na-concentration in NM exhibits two prominent phases with a gradual change in the crystal lattice. The solid-solution domains reveal a gradual increase in lattice parameter <i>c</i> by about 1.3% until <i>x</i>=0.5, and after that, there are no significant changes in the lattice parameters. Additionally, the a/b ratio and <i>d</i>-spacings were calculated and are presented in FigureS10, showing a ratio close to 1.73, indicative of transformation towards more symmetric hexagonal lattice. The refinements are presented in the Supporting Information along with simulated diffraction patterns (FigureS8). Future, synchrotron and neutron studies may be required to comprehend the detailed structural changes.</p>
            
            <p>We have investigated the long voltage plateaus during the first charge around 2.7V and multiple small voltage plateaus before completing the full charge at 4.2V. These PITT measurements were carried at the rate of C/50 with a 5mV voltage pulse for 1h rest to understand the definitiveness of each plateau. It also provides the amount of sodium deintercalated/intercalated from/into the structure during the operation. In Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4g</a> and <a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4h</a>, we show the current response at each voltage pulse. As soon as deintercalation of sodium starts in NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, there is a sharp rise in the current response to its maximum within 11min and then relaxes after 360min. This current response is typical of a two-phase reaction with a significantly higher phase transformation barrier, akin to a first order phase transition.</p>
            
            <p>In PITT, the sharp rise in current significantly impacts structural stability. However, the initial transition at 2.7V in the NM sample is gradual, resembling a symmetric parabola with gradual bump and relaxation. Additionally, <i>Operando</i> XRD reveals that the initial composition differs from subsequent cycles, consistently lacking around 0.16 Na. A maximum of 0.85Na was deintercalated from NM at the end of full charge (4.2V). The PITT curves for NA and NC samples are presented in FigureS11, which display sharp transitions during the first charge, similar to NaNiO<sub>2</sub>. To delve into the influence of the initial transition, PITT was extended to the second cycle. The initial bi-phasic transformation in the subsequent cycles is not as sharp as in the first cycle. However, it is still notable in NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, NA, and NC. In contrast, this biphasic domain almost disappeared in NM. Overall, both <i>operando</i> XRD and PITT proved instrumental in enhancing our understanding of the phase transformations occurring during cycling. FigureS12 illustrates the GITT curves for the four cathodes, with the diffusion coefficients calculated using The Weppner and Huggins modified formula.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0034_R_d101962782e1459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">34</a></sup></span> Further details are provided in the supporting note2. The calculated diffusion coefficients for the first charge and discharge curves are plotted against time in FigureS12 and S13, revealing variations between 10<sup>13</sup> and 10<sup>9</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>. The average diffusion coefficients for NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, NA, NM, and NC are, respectively, 3.1410<sup>12</sup>, 3.210<sup>12</sup>, 5.510<sup>12</sup> and 5.1710<sup>12</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>. Randles-Sevick's equation was also used to calculate the diffusion coefficient with the cyclic voltammetry data,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0035_R_d101962782e1486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Faraday Discuss.">35</a></sup></span> confirming the consistency in the GITT calculations (supporting note 3, FigureS14 and S15).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202403865-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202403865-sec-0007-title"> Magic Angle Spinning Nuclear Magnetic Resonance Spectroscopy (MASNMR ).</h3>
            
            <p><i>Operando</i> X-ray diffraction undoubtedly demonstrated the influence of the dopants in controlling the phase transformations by perturbing the long-range ordering. However, it has been largely proven that the local structure plays a critical role in enhancing the electrochemical properties and mitigating cooperative JahnTeller distortion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0008_R_d101962782e1500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0009_R_d101962782e1500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span> MASNMR is an essential technique to probe the local environment of NMR active nuclei to identify composition dependent variations within the electronic structure of cathode materials. The <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra for undoped NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and doped cathodes were investigated under a 9.4T field (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5a</a>), highlighting the strong paramagnetic and Fermi-contact shift interactions between the unpaired electrons (Ni<sup>3+</sup>) and sodium ions leading to an extremely wide chemical shift range and large degree of chemical shift anisotropy (CSA) within the NaNiO<sub>2</sub> cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0009_R_d101962782e1515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span> While the site-specific chemical information provided by the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS spectra at these spinning rates are obscured by spinning sidebands (SSB), variable spinning experiments were conducted to investigate the CSA tensor of these materials. Line fitting of the pristine NaNiO<sub>2</sub> sample was performed with <i>DMfit</i> software to extract the chemical shift (=1,465ppm) and CSA parameters (<sub>CSA</sub>=521.8ppm and <sub>CSA</sub>=0.488) (FigureS16). With the incorporation of the dopants, the CSA tensor analysis becomes untenable, especially in the NM sample, as a result of SSB overlap of the different sites.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202403865-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebd7e4cc-62e8-424b-a93e-d270c6df7c40/anie202403865-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebd7e4cc-62e8-424b-a93e-d270c6df7c40/anie202403865-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9d83f94d-b2a3-48d0-8cfd-5250e4e46f7b/anie202403865-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebd7e4cc-62e8-424b-a93e-d270c6df7c40/anie202403865-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403865-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403865" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR of as-synthesized cathodes. (b) pj-MATPASS 2D spectra of the NaNiO<sub>2</sub> cathode powder and (c) the corresponding projections of the cathode powder. (d) Comparison of <sup>23</sup>Na deconvoluted MAS NMR spectra of the NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NM samples. (e) charged to 4.2V and (f) discharged to 1.8V. (g) Simplified representation of local environments in distorted crystal structures.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The <sup>23</sup>Na pj-MATPASS technique was employed to identify the isotropic chemical shifts (<sub>iso</sub>) of different sodium environments within the as-prepared cathode materials as shown in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5b</a><a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">c</a>. The F2 sheared pj-MATPASS spectra of the NM sample is shown in FigureS17. With the incorporation of Al<sup>3+</sup> ions into the structure, the main peak shifts to 1,475ppm and is accompanied by a prominent shoulder at 1,498ppm (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5c</a>). In addition, new modes arise in the isotropic NA spectra centered between 1,250 and 1,554ppm, assigned to Na in close proximity to 1 or more AlO<sub>6</sub> octahedral sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0036_R_d101962782e1581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0038_R_d101962782e1581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span> Similarly, with the incorporation of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>3+</sup>, a greater shift of the main mode is observed in both NM (1,528ppm) and NC (1,530ppm), but these two samples exhibit a much greater full width at half maximum (FWHM) than the NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NA samples, which arises from the lack of ordering in the Na planes. In the case of NC, additional broad modes arise at 970 and 1,440ppm as a result of diamagnetic Co<sup>3+</sup> in the Na(ONi<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>6x</sub>(OCo<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>x</sub> environment.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0039_R_d101962782e1601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">39</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>In the as-synthesized NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, the dominant signal is centered at ~1,440ppm (95%), and is accompanied by a shoulder at ~1,330ppm (5%), assigned to sodium in octahedral Na(ONi<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>6</sub> sites and octahedral Na sites near Na vacancies and stacking faults where the local electron density is reduced. (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5d</a>). The relative populations of these two sites were extracted with gaussian fitting and is consistent with the Rietveld refinement of the pristine NaNiO<sub>2</sub> sample (See Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#anie202403865-tbl-0001">1</a>). In comparison, the deconvolution of the NM pj-MATPASS spectra reveals four broad modes at (895, 1,190, 1,440, and 1,735ppm), as a result of the disproportionation of paramagnetic Ni<sup>3+</sup> to strongly paramagnetic Ni<sup>2+</sup> and diamagnetic Ni<sup>4+</sup>. The primary mode is centered at 1,440ppm and is tentatively assigned to Na(ONi<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>6</sub> sites, which make up ~40% of the sodium population, see (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5g</a>). Unique to the NM sample, the primary mode is accompanied by a shoulder centered at ~1,735ppm (25%), which is tentatively assigned to a more paramagnetic environment, such as a Na(ONi<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>6x</sub>(ONi<sup>2+</sup>)<sub>x</sub> site. Two new modes also arise at lower chemical shift values, specifically at 895ppm (5%) and 1,190ppm (30%), which have been attributed to Na(ONi<sup>3+</sup>)<sub>6x</sub>(OMg)<sub>x</sub> octahedral sites where x is either 1 or 2 in the formula (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5g</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0037_R_d101962782e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">37</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0038_R_d101962782e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span> The decreased chemical shift can be attributed to an increase in the ionicity of the TMO bonds, and a reduction of the Fermi-contact shift on the transference of unpaired magnetic moments through the oxygen atoms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0040_R_d101962782e1656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR of the cathodes after charging to 4.2V is shown in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5e</a>, revealing a series of Na environments dependent on the resulting structure. <i>Operando</i> XRD confirms that NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NA both retain a mixture of the O3 type and O3 type structures, and as such, the modes present at 840 and 870ppm can be assigned, respectively, to octahedral and prismatic Na coordinated by a distribution of Ni<sup>3+/4+</sup> (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5g</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0038_R_d101962782e1677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span> Consistent with the electrochemical results, the large shift in the peak position from ~1,440ppm (as-synthesized) to ~850ppm (charged) can be attributed to the increased concentration of diamagnetic Ni<sup>4+</sup> due to the extraction of sodium. Interestingly, <i>operando</i> XRD shows NM adopts exclusively P3 type structures upon charging to 4.2V, but the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum reveals the presence of a small portion of Na octahedra (~800ppm) with a majority of Na occupying prismatic (&lt;800ppm) environments, which are consistent with the reports by Kalapsazova etal. on the P3Na<sub>0.5</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub> phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0039_R_d101962782e1691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">39</a></sup></span> In the charged NM cathode, sodium largely occupies prismatic environments, but in the charged NC cathode, a mixture of octahedral (~800ppm) and prismatic (657ppm) sites are observed.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0008_R_d101962782e1694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0009_R_d101962782e1694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0038_R_d101962782e1694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>While charging Ni<sup>4+</sup> is reduced to Ni<sup>3+</sup>, increasing the concentration of unpaired electrons near sodium. Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR was conducted on cathodes after discharging to 1.8V as shown in Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0005">5f</a>. Consistent with the as-prepared cathodes, the spectra of the discharged cathodes reveal the presence of broad modes, centered between 500 and 1,400ppm. While this highlights the exceptional reversibility of sodium intercalation upon cycling, the center of gravity of the peaks in the discharged-state spectra do not return to their original positions (~1,500ppm), indicating a minor loss of Na during the first cycle. Consistent with <i>operando</i> XRD (Figure<a href="#anie202403865-fig-0004">4</a>), the cycled cathode contains a higher concentration of Ni<sup>4+</sup> and thus a lower concentration of Ni<sup>3+</sup> and corresponding paramagnetic contribution.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0040_R_d101962782e1718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> It should be noted that the sharp peak centered near 0ppm in all samples is attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> ions in a diamagnetic environment, presumably these are from residual electrolyte or CEI products formed during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0017_R_d101962782e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0038_R_d101962782e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#anie202403865-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0040_R_d101962782e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> A simplified phase diagram based on the first cycle of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> and NM with respect to the Na content is presented in FigureS18 (supporting note 4). Additionally, the O3-type Ni rich cathodes have been summarized in TableS3 of the Supporting Information.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403865-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403865-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, Mg<sup>2+</sup> dopant efficiently alleviates the multiple biphasic transformations during sodium intercalation/deintercalation. <i>Operando</i> X-ray diffractograms demonstrated smooth transitions between charging and discharging for the Mg<sup>2+</sup>-doped sample. Rietveld analyses along with sequential refinement suggested long-range solid-solution domains with ordering at <i>x</i>=0.66 (P3Na<sub>0.66</sub>NM) and <i>x</i>=0.5 (P3Na<sub>0.5</sub>NM) during the first cycle. This resulted from an effective reduction of cooperative JahnTeller distortion (from Van Vleck distortion modes) and stronger ionic interactions between Mg<sup>2+</sup> and O<sup>2</sup>, deshielding the electronic density from Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Consequently, the <i>d</i>-spacing increased, allowing a wider space for the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Structural analysis coupled with bond-valence site energy calculations, illustrated possible iso-surface pathways and lower migration barrier for the Mg-doped sample. MASNMR spectroscopy provided insights into local structure and how the paramagnetic interactions of Ni atoms perturb and effectively reduce the cooperative JahnTeller distortion. The excellent electrochemistry of Mg-doped sample was evident, with ~96% capacity retention over the 100cycles. The understanding gained in this work can stimulate further work to bring NaNiO<sub>2</sub>-based cathodes for NIBs on par with the LiNiO<sub>2</sub>-based cathodes for LIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403865-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403865-sec-0009-title"> Supporting Information</h2>
         
         <p>Detailed procedure for synthesis and characterization, enlarged XRD patterns, TGA data, Rietveld analysis of <i>operando</i> XRD patterns, simulated patterns, SEM-EDX, <i>operando</i> XRD, electrochemistry, GITT, cyclic voltammetry, variable spinning-rate analysis NMR, CSA analysis, table with distortions of Van Vleck modes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403865-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0041_R_d101962782e1782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a>-<a href="#anie202403865-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403865-bib-0048_R_d101962782e1782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater.          </section>
